Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Reclaiming Slave and Goods from Dar al-Harb
Intra-Hanafi dispute on combined slave and goods capture

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on reclaiming a slave who escapes with property, and explain the basis of their difference.

Core Idea
A slave's inviolability persists even in enemy hands, but property does not — the dispute turns on whether combined purchase alters the rule.

This passage from the Hidayah presents a nuanced intra-Hanafi disagreement. When a slave flees to enemy territory with a horse and goods, and a Muslim purchases the entire bundle, does the original master reclaim the slave for free? Imam Abu Hanifa says yes, because the slave's personal inviolability is not erased by capture. The Sahibayn analogize the combined sale to the individual items, requiring payment for all. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply legal principles across different scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the legal question
Read the passage. A slave escapes with a horse and goods to Dar al-Harb. Harbis capture everything. A Muslim buys the bundle and brings it to Dar al-Islam. Does the original master reclaim the slave for free or must he pay?
2
State Imam Abu Hanifa's position and his rationale
Imam Abu Hanifa holds the master takes the slave for free, but must pay for the horse and goods. His implied rationale: the slave retains personal inviolability (hurmat), so the harbi purchaser never gains valid ownership of the slave; goods lack this inviolability.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their analogical reasoning
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold the master must pay the price for all items, including the slave. They analogize the combined purchase to the separate rulings for each item, considering the combined state as analogous to the individual cases.
4
Determine the practical consequence and the preferred view
The thamarah al-khilaf: if the master cannot pay, Abu Hanifa still gets the slave free; Sahibayn require payment for everything, so the master loses the slave too. The author (al-Marghinani) favors Abu Hanifa’s view, which is the mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنْ أَبَقَ عَبْدٌ إِلَيْهِمْ وَذَهَبَ مَعَهُ بِفَرَسٍ وَمَتَاعٍ فَأَخَذَ الْمُشْرِكُونَ ذَلِكَ كُلَّهُ فَاشْتَرَى رَجُلٌ ذَلِكَ كُلَّهُ وَأَخْرَجَهُ إِلَى دَارِ الْإِسْلَامِ فَإِنَّ الْمَوْلَى يَأْخُذُ الْعَبْدَ بِغَيْرِ شَيْءٍ وَالْفَرَسَ وَالْمَتَاعَ بِالثَّمَنِ وَهَذَا عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَقَالَا يَأْخُذَانِ الْعَبْدَ وَمَا مَعَهُ بِالثَّمَنِ إِنْ شَاءَ اعْتِبَارَ الْحَالَةِ الْإِجْتِمَاعِيَّةِ بِحَالَةِ الْإِنْفِرَادِ وَقَدْ بَيَّنَّا الْحُكْمَ فِي كُلِّ فَرْدٍ ترجمہ اور اگر ہمارا کوئی غلام اپنے ساتھ گھوڑا اور کچھ اسباب بھی لے کر ان مشرکوں کے پاس پہنچا اور ان لوگوں نے اس غلام کو اس کے جانور اور سامان کے ساتھ گرفتار کر لیا۔ پھر کسی مسلمان نے ان تمام چیزوں غلام، جانور اور سامان کو ایک ساتھ خرید لیا اور دارالاسلام لے آیا۔ تو اس صورت میں اس کا پرانا مالک غلام کو تو مفت میں یعنی کسی قیمت کی ادائیگی کے بغیر واپس لے لے گا۔ البتہ اس گھوڑے اور اس کے سامان کو ان کی قیمت دے کر لے سکتا ہے۔ یہ قول امام ابوحنیفہ رحمۃ اللہ علیہ کا ہے۔ لیکن صاحبین رحمۃ اللہ علیہما نے کہا ہے کہ اگر چاہے تو گھوڑے اور سامان کے ساتھ غلام کو بھی ان کی قیمت ادا کر کے واپس لے سکتا ہے۔ یہ حکم دراصل ان چیزوں کو ایک ساتھ کر کے ایک ایک چیز پر قیاس ہے۔ جبکہ ہم نے پہلے ہی ایک ایک مسئلہ کا حکم بیان کر دیا ہے۔ ( یعنی تنہا غلام کے بھاگنے میں یہی حکم ہے تو گھوڑا اور اسباب کے ساتھ بھاگنے کا بھی یہی حکم ہے۔)
If a slave runs away to them and goes with a horse and goods, and the polytheists take all of that, then a man buys all of that and takes it out to Dar al-Islam, then the master takes the slave for nothing (free) and the horse and goods for the price. This is according to Abu Hanifa. And they (the two) said: He (the master) may take the slave and what is with him for the price if he wishes, considering the combined state as the separate state. And we have already explained the ruling for each individual item. Translation: And if a slave of ours, along with a horse and some goods, reaches these polytheists, and they capture the slave along with the animal and goods. Then a Muslim buys all these things—the slave, animal, and goods—together and brings them to Dar al-Islam. In this case, his original master will take the slave for free, i.e., without paying any price. However, he can take the horse and the goods by paying their price. This is the statement of Imam Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him). But the two Companions (Sahibayn) (may Allah have mercy on them) said that if he wishes, he may take the slave along with the horse and goods by paying their price. This ruling is essentially analogizing the combined situation to the isolated individual items. And we have already stated the ruling for each individual matter. (That is, the ruling for the slave running away alone is the same; the ruling for running away with a horse and goods is also the same.)
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a scenario where a slave escapes with a horse and goods to Dar al-Harb. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the original master can reclaim the slave for free (as the slave regains inviolability), but must pay for the horse and goods (since they lack inviolability). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) disagree, holding that the master must pay for everything, including the slave. They analogize the combined purchase to the separate rulings for each item, considering that the master should pay the price for all if he wishes to reclaim them. The commentary explains that this is a qiyas (analogy) from the individual cases to the combined one, and the author notes that the ruling for each individual item was previously explained.

Key Terms
صاحبین (Sahibayn): Refers to Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, the two main disciples of Imam Abu Hanifa.
قیاس (Qiyas): Analogical reasoning, here applied to consider the combined case as analogous to individual cases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the combined case of a slave escaping with a horse and goods, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, what does the original master take for free?
A) The horse only
B) The slave only
C) The goods only
D) All items

Key Takeaway
Imam Abu Hanifa distinguishes between the inviolable slave and property; the Sahibayn analogize across the board, requiring payment for all.

The Harbi and the Muslim Slave: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a Muslim slave becomes free when taken to Dar al-Harb by a harbi with aman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the emancipation of a Muslim slave taken to Dar al-Harb by a harbi with aman, and explain the legal reasoning behind each view.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: rescuing a Muslim from kafir slavery is wajib; Sahibayn: ownership transfer requires a precise method.

This passage presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: a harbi (non-Muslim enemy) enters Dar al-Islam under aman (safe conduct), buys a Muslim slave, and then takes the slave into Dar al-Harb (enemy territory). Does the slave become free upon entering Dar al-Harb? Imam Abu Hanifa rules 'yes', while the two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), rule 'no'. Their disagreement reveals a deeper tension between teleological goals (rescuing a Muslim from humiliation) and procedural formalism (ownership transfer by specific means). By working through this dispute, you will learn how Hanafi jurists balance principles and methods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the parties
Understand the key actors: a harbi enters Dar al-Islam with aman, purchases a Muslim slave, and takes that slave to Dar al-Harb. The ruling is contested between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.
Aman gives the harbi temporary protection, but does it grant full ownership rights over a Muslim slave?
2
State the two rulings
Abu Hanifa: the slave becomes free upon entering Dar al-Harb. Sahibayn: the slave remains a slave.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is whether the slave can be reclaimed or becomes a free Muslim.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof
Abu Hanifa argues that rescuing a Muslim from kafir slavery is wajib (obligatory). Since the harbi is no longer in Dar al-Islam, the authority to compel a sale (jabr) is lost. However, the change of territory (the condition) can stand in for the missing cause (emancipation). He analogizes to the case of spouses in Dar al-Harb: if one converts, the passage of three menstrual periods acts as divorce to free the Muslim from a mixed-faith marriage.
This is a qiyas khafi (subtle analogy) – the condition substitutes for the cause based on the overriding principle of rescue.
4
Analyze the Sahibayn's proof
The Sahibayn hold that the harbi's ownership can only be removed by a specific method: compulsory sale (jabr). Once the harbi leaves Dar al-Islam, the authority to compel him is gone, so the ownership remains intact. The slave does not become free.
They prioritize procedural certainty over the teleological goal of rescue.
5
Determine the practical consequence and the preferred view
According to Abu Hanifa, the slave is free; according to the Sahibayn, he remains a slave and could be purchased back if possible. The author of al-Hidayah seems to favor Abu Hanifa's view, making it the mufta bihi (operative ruling).
This illustrates that in Hanafi usul, when the method fails, the underlying purpose (maqasid) can override the specific procedure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا دَخَلَ الْحَرْبِيُّ دَارَنَا بِأَمَانٍ وَاشْتَرَى عَبْدًا مُسْلِمًا وَأَدْخَلَهُ دَارَ الْحَرْبِ عَتَقَ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَقَالَا لَا يَعْتِقُ لِأَنَّ الْإِزَالَةَ كَانَتْ مُسْتَحَقَّةً بِطَرِيقٍ مُعَيَّنٍ وَهُوَ الْبَيْعُ وَقَدِ انْقَطَعَتْ وِلَايَةُ الْجَبْرِ عَلَيْهِ فَبَقِيَ فِي يَدِهِ عَبْدًا وَلِأَبِي حَنِيفَةَ أَنَّ تَخْلِيصَ الْمُسْلِمِ عَنْ ذُلِّ الْكَافِرِ وَاجِبٌ فَيُقَامُ الشَّرْطُ وَهُوَ تَبَايُنُ الدَّارَيْنِ مَقَامَ الْعِلَّةِ وَهُوَ الْإِعْتَاقُ تَخْلِيصًا لَهُ كَمَا يُقَامُ مُضِيُّ ثَلَاثِ حَيْضٍ مَقَامَ التَّفْرِيقِ فِيمَا إِذَا أَسْلَمَ أَحَدُ الزَّوْجَيْنِ فِي دَارِ الْحَرْبِ ترجمہ اوراگر کوئی حربی اجازت اور امان لے کر ہمارے دار الاسلام میں آیا اور وہ یہاں سے کسی مسلمان یا ذمی غلام کو خرید کر اپنے ساتھ دار الحرب لے گیا۔ تو امام ابوحنیفہ رحمۃ اللہ علیہ نے فرمایا ہے کہ وہ غلام وہاں جاتے ہی آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ لیکن صاحبین رحمۃ اللہ علیہما نے فرمایا ہے کہ وہ آزاد نہیں ہوگا ۔ کیونکہ حربی کافر کی ملکیت کو ایک خاص طور پر یعنی بطور بیع کے دور کرنا واجب تھا ( ۔ یعنی اس حربی کافر کو اس بات پر مجبور کیا جاتا کہ مسلمان غلام کو وہ فروخت کر دے۔ پھر اگر وہ انکار کرتا تو قاضی جبراً اس غلام کو فروخت کر کے اس کی قیمت اس حربی کو دے دیتا) لیکن موجودہ صورت میں اس حربی پر جبر کرنے کا اختیار باقی نہ رہا۔ اس لئے یہ غلام اس کے پاس غلام ہی کے حکم میں رہ گیا اور امام ابوحنیفہ رحمۃ اللہ علیہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ ایک مسلمان کو کافر کی غلامی کی ذلت سے نکالنا واجب ہے۔ اس لئے شرط یعنی دونوں ملکوں کی جدائی کو علت کے قائم مقام قرار دیا جائے گا۔ یعنی آزاد کرنا۔ تا کہ وہ غلام اپنی ذات کی زندگی سے چھوٹ جائے ۔ جیسے اس صورت میں کہ عورت یا اس کا شوہر دار الحرب میں مسلمان ہو گیا تو تین حیض گزر جانے کو ہی طلاق دینے کے قائم مقام کر دیا جائے گا تاکہ مسلمان بیوی اپنے کافر شوہر یا مسلمان شوہر اپنی کافر بیوی کے ساتھ ناپاکی کی زندگی سے نجات پالے)
And when a harbi enters our territory with aman and buys a Muslim slave and takes him into Dar al-Harb, the slave becomes free according to Abu Hanifa. And they (Sahibayn) said: He does not become free, because the removal (of his ownership) was due by a specific method, i.e., sale, and the authority of compulsion over him has ceased, so he remains a slave in his hand. And for Abu Hanifa: freeing a Muslim from the humiliation of the kafir is obligatory. So the condition, which is the difference of the two territories, is placed in the position of the cause, which is emancipation to free him, just as the passage of three menstrual periods is placed in the position of separation in the case when one of the spouses accepts Islam in Dar al-Harb. Translation: And if a harbi enters our Dar al-Islam with permission and aman, and buys a Muslim or dhimmi slave from here and takes him with him to Dar al-Harb, then Imam Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) said that the slave will become free upon reaching there. But the two Companions (Sahibayn) (may Allah have mercy on them) said that he will not become free. Because it was obligatory to remove the harbi's ownership in a specific way, i.e., by sale. (That is, the harbi would be compelled to sell the Muslim slave. If he refused, the judge would forcibly sell the slave and give the price to the harbi.) But in the present case, the authority to compel the harbi no longer exists. Therefore, this slave remains as a slave in his possession. And the proof of Imam Abu Hanifa is that it is obligatory to rescue a Muslim from the humiliation of slavery under a kafir. Therefore, the condition, i.e., the separation of the two territories, is placed in the position of the cause, i.e., emancipation, so that the slave may be freed from his situation. Just as the passage of three menstruations is placed in the position of divorce in the case where one of the spouses becomes Muslim in Dar al-Harb, so that the Muslim wife may be saved from her kafir husband or the Muslim husband from his kafir wife.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a harbi who enters Dar al-Islam under aman, buys a Muslim slave, and takes him to Dar al-Harb. Imam Abu Hanifa rules that the slave becomes free upon entering Dar al-Harb. His reasoning is that it is wajib (obligatory) to rescue a Muslim from the humiliation of non-Muslim slavery. Therefore, the condition of the territories being different (Dar al-Harb vs. Dar al-Islam) is treated as the effective cause for emancipation, even though the harbi did not intend to free him. He draws an analogy to the case where a married couple is in Dar al-Harb and one spouse converts: the passage of three menstrual periods is treated as divorce to allow the Muslim to escape a mixed-faith marriage. The Sahibayn disagree, arguing that the harbi's ownership can only be removed through a specific process (compulsory sale), and since that process is not possible once he leaves Dar al-Islam, the slave remains a slave. The text thus shows a difference in approach: Abu Hanifa prioritizes the principle of rescuing a Muslim, while Sahibayn adhere to the procedural method of ownership transfer.

Key Terms
امان (Aman): Safe conduct or temporary protection granted to a non-Muslim enemy allowing him to enter Dar al-Islam safely.
جبر (Jabr): Compulsion, specifically the authority of the Muslim ruler to compel a harbi to sell a Muslim slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what is the reason for the slave becoming free when taken to Dar al-Harb by a harbi with aman?
A) The harbi's purchase is invalid because of the aman.
B) It is obligatory to rescue a Muslim from the humiliation of kafir slavery, and the separation of territories acts as the cause of emancipation.
C) The slave's master in Dar al-Islam recovers ownership.
D) The harbi loses his right of ownership automatically.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when the prescribed method cannot be executed, the overarching principle of rescuing a Muslim may allow a condition to substitute for the cause, but such substitution is contested even within the school.

Reclaiming a Muslim Slave from Dar al-Harb: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on reclaiming a Muslim slave bought from dar al-harb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on reclaiming a Muslim slave bought back from dar al-harb and apply the principle of i'tibar al-halah al-ijtima'iyyah bi-halah al-infirādiyyah.

Core Idea
For Abu Hanifa, the slave returns free; goods require payment. Sahibayn require payment for both.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute. When a Muslim slave escapes to enemy territory with a horse and goods, and a Muslim purchases the entire bundle and brings it to dar al-Islam, should the original master pay to get his slave back? Imam Abu Hanifa frees the slave without compensation, but his two disciples disagree, drawing an analogy to the rule for each item individually. Understanding their reasoning sharpens your grasp of how Hanafi jurists balance individual rights and the higher objective of freeing Muslims from dar al-harb.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Precisely describe the facts: a Muslim slave flees to dar al-harb with a horse and goods. The polytheists capture him. Later, a Muslim purchases the slave, horse, and goods together and brings them to dar al-Islam. The original master now claims the slave and the goods.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
According to Abu Hanifa, the master takes the slave without paying anything, but must pay the price to get the horse and goods. Why? Because the slave's freedom from kufr is paramount, while the goods are mere property.
Abu Hanifa distinguishes between the slave and the goods.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the master may take the slave, horse, and goods all by paying the purchase price, if he wishes. They argue by analogy: just as a purchased slave alone would require payment, so too when goods are added, the same rule applies.
They apply the principle: 'considering the combined state as the individual state' (i'tibar al-halah al-ijtima'iyyah bi-halah al-infirādiyyah).
4
Analyse the reasoning principle
The Sahibayn use qiyas: each item individually would be reclaimed by paying the price; therefore the bundle should also be reclaimed by paying the price. Abu Hanifa's implicit counter is that the slave's freedom is a superior consideration that overrides this analogy.
This is a talil (reasoning) dispute about the scope of analogical reasoning.
5
Identify the practical difference
Under Abu Hanifa, the master recovers his slave at no cost, encouraging freeing Muslims from polytheist control. Under Sahibayn, financial burden may deter the master from reclaiming the slave.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلى دَارِ الإِسْلَامِ فَإِنَّ الْمَوْلى يَأْخُذُ الْعَبْدَ بِغَيْرِ شَيْءٍ وَالْفَرَسَ وَالْمَتَاعَ بِالثَّمَنِ وَهَذَا عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَقَالَا يَأْخُذَانِ الْعَبْدَ وَمَا مَعَهُ بِالثَّمَنِ إِنْ شَاءَ اعْتِبَارُ الْحَالَةِ الْإِجْتِمَاعِيَّةِ بِحَالَةِ الْإِنْفِرَادِيَّةِ وَقَدْ بَيَّنَّا الْحُكْمَ فِي كُلِّ فَرْدٍ
To dar al-Islam: then the master takes the slave without anything, and the horse and the goods for the price. This is according to Abu Hanifa. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: They take the slave and what is with him for the price if he wishes, considering the collective state as the individual state. And we have already explained the ruling for each individual item.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a Muslim slave who escapes to dar al-harb with a horse and goods, is captured by the polytheists, and then a Muslim purchases the entire bundle (slave, horse, and goods) and brings it back to dar al-Islam. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the original master may reclaim his slave without paying any price (i.e., the slave is returned for free), but he must pay the price to retrieve the horse and goods. The reasoning is that the slave's own freedom is paramount, and the goods are considered separate acquisitions. However, the two companions (Sahibayn—Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) hold that the original master may choose to also reclaim the slave by paying the price, along with the horse and goods. They analogize the combined situation to the individual case: since the slave alone when purchased would have required payment, adding goods does not change the rule. The text notes that the ruling for each item individually has already been explained.

Key Terms
مَوْلَى (mawla): The original master/owner of the slave before his escape.
الثَّمَن (al-thaman): The price paid to the Muslim buyer to reclaim the slave or goods.
اعْتِبَارُ الْحَالَةِ الْإِجْتِمَاعِيَّةِ بِحَالَةِ الْإِنْفِرَادِيَّةِ (i'tibar al-halah al-ijtima'iyyah bi-halah al-infirādiyyah): Analogizing the combined situation (slave with goods) to the individual situation (each item alone).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what does the original master receive for free when a Muslim purchases a fleeing Muslim slave along with his horse and goods and brings them to dar al-Islam?
A) The horse and goods
B) The slave only
C) Both the slave and the horse
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
Imam Abu Hanifa returns the Muslim slave without compensation to encourage freeing him from enemy hands, while the Sahibayn require payment for the entire bundle based on analogy with individual items.

8 min
Does the Muslim Slave Become Free?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a Muslim slave becomes free when taken to dar al-harb by a harbi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding a Muslim slave taken to dar al-harb, and identify the legal reasoning and its practical consequence.

Core Idea
A Muslim slave becomes free automatically when taken to dar al-harb? Abu Hanifa says yes, Sahibayn say no.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute on the status of a Muslim slave who is purchased by a harbi (non-Muslim from enemy territory) under aman and then taken to dar al-harb. The key question: does the slave become free upon entering dar al-harb? Abu Hanifa argues yes based on the obligation to rescue a Muslim from humiliation, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) argue no, because the specific means of compulsion (sale) is no longer enforceable. We will analyze the proofs and the legal analogy that underpins Abu Hanifa's position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
Understand the facts: a harbi with aman buys a Muslim slave in dar al-Islam and then takes the slave to dar al-harb. The question is the slave's status.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa holds that the slave becomes free (i'taq). His proof: it is wajib to rescue a Muslim from the humiliation of kufr; the condition of separation of the two abodes (tabayun al-darayn) serves as a substitute for the act of manumission (i'taq). He draws an analogy: just as the passage of three menstrual periods stands in place of divorce when one spouse embraces Islam in dar al-harb, here the separation of abodes stands for manumission.
The analogy is key: condition substitutes for cause when the cause is impossible to implement.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and proof
The Sahibayn disagree: the slave does not become free. Their proof: the removal of ownership was required through a specific means – sale (the qadi's authority to compel the harbi to sell). Once the harbi leaves dar al-Islam, the Muslim judge's authority (wilayat al-jabr) is lost. Therefore, the harbi retains ownership and the slave remains enslaved.
The Sahibayn focus on the practical loss of jurisdiction.
4
Analyze the core difference: condition as substitute vs. limitation of authority
The dispute hinges on whether a legal condition can replace a cause when the cause is no longer possible. Abu Hanifa treats the obligation to rescue as overriding the procedural limitation. The Sahibayn maintain that the specific cause (sale) cannot be replaced by a condition once the authority is lost.
This is a methodological difference in qiyas and ta'lil.
5
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa's view, the slave instantly becomes a free Muslim upon crossing into dar al-harb. Under Sahibayn, the slave remains in the harbi's ownership and must be ransomed or bought back by Muslims. The ruling affects the slave's personal status and any further interactions.
Knowing the mufta bihi position requires checking later tarjih; the text does not specify.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا دَخَلَ الْحَرْبِيُّ دَارَنَا بِأَمَانٍ وَاشْتَرَى عَبْدًا مُسْلِمًا وَأَدْخَلَهُ دَارَ الْحَرْبِ عَتَقَ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَقَالَا لَا يَعْتِقُ لِأَنَّ الْإِزَالَةَ كَانَتْ مُسْتَحَقَّةً بِطَرِيقٍ مُعَيَّنٍ وَهُوَ الْبَيْعُ وَقَدِ انْقَطَعَتْ وَلَايَةُ الْجَبْرِ عَلَيْهِ فَبَقِيَ فِي يَدِهِ عَبْدًا وَلِأَبِي حَنِيفَةَ أَنَّ تَخْلِيصَ الْمُسْلِمِ عَنْ ذُلِّ الْكَافِرِ وَاجِبٌ فَيُقَامُ الشَّرْطُ وَهُوَ تَبَايُنُ الدَّارَيْنِ مَقَامَ الْعِلَّةِ وَهُوَ الْإِعْتَاقُ تَخْلِيصًا لَهُ كَمَا يُقَامُ مُضِيُّ ثَلَاثِ حِيَضٍ مَقَامَ التَّفْرِيقِ فِيمَا إِذَا أَسْلَمَ أَحَدُ الزَّوْجَيْنِ فِي دَارِ الْحَرْبِ
And when a harbi enters our dar with aman and buys a Muslim slave and takes him to dar al-harb, he becomes free according to Abu Hanifa. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He does not become free because the removal (of ownership) was required by a specific means, namely sale, and the authority of compulsion (wilayat al-jabr) has been cut off over him, so he remains a slave in his hand. And for Abu Hanifa (his proof) is that rescuing the Muslim from the humiliation of the disbeliever is obligatory, so the condition, which is the difference of the two abodes (tabayun al-darayn), is set up in the place of the cause, which is manumission (itaq), to rescue him, just as the passage of three menstruations is set up in the place of separation when one of the spouses becomes Muslim in dar al-harb.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage addresses the case where a harbi (non-Muslim from dar al-harb) enters dar al-Islam with a safe passage (aman), purchases a Muslim slave, and then takes the slave back to dar al-harb. Imam Abu Hanifa rules that the slave becomes free upon entering dar al-harb because it is obligatory (wajib) to rescue a Muslim from the degradation of living under a disbeliever's ownership. Since direct manumission by force is no longer possible once the harbi returns to his territory, the legal condition of the separation of the two abodes (tabayun al-darayn) serves as a substitute for the actual act of freeing. The Sahibayn disagree: they argue that the method of removing ownership was specifically determined as sale (i.e., the harbi was compelled to sell the slave while in dar al-Islam), but once he leaves, the Muslim authorities no longer have jurisdiction (wilayat al-jabr) to enforce this. Therefore, the slave remains in the harbi's ownership as before. Abu Hanifa supports his view by drawing an analogy to the case where one spouse becomes Muslim in dar al-harb: the passage of three menstrual periods (mudiy thalath hiyad) stands in place of a formal divorce to end the marriage, similarly, here the separation of abodes stands in place of manumission.

Key Terms
وَلَايَةُ الْجَبْرِ (wilayat al-jabr): The authority of compulsion exercised by a Muslim judge (qadi) over a harbi within dar al-Islam to enforce sale of a Muslim slave.
تَبَايُنُ الدَّارَيْنِ (tabayun al-darayn): The legal separation between dar al-Islam and dar al-harb, used as a trigger for certain rulings like manumission or marital dissolution.
إِعْتَاق (i'taq): Manumission; the act of freeing a slave. Here, Abu Hanifa treats the condition (tabayun) as standing in place of the actual act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for Abu Hanifa's ruling that the Muslim slave becomes free when taken to dar al-harb?
A) The harbi violated the aman
B) It is obligatory to rescue a Muslim from humiliation of kufr
C) The slave's purchase was invalid
D) The harbi lost ownership by leaving dar al-Islam

Key Takeaway
The dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn over the slave's freedom illustrates a deeper methodological question: can a legal condition replace an impossible cause when the obligation is wajib?

Harbi's Slave Becomes Muslim and Comes to Dar al-Islam
Ruling with proof that a harbi's slave becomes free upon embracing Islam and reaching Muslim territory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a harbi's slave becomes free upon embracing Islam and reaching Muslim territory, and explain the proof from the hadith of the slaves of Ta'if and the rational argument of yad.

Core Idea
A slave who embraces Islam and escapes to Muslim land is automatically free — no formal manumission needed.

This passage presents a rule from the Hanafi law of siyar: when a slave belonging to a non-Muslim harbi embraces Islam and then either flees to dar al-Islam or the Muslims conquer the territory, the slave becomes free. The ruling is based on a prophetic precedent and a rational principle about self-control. Let's walk through the proof and reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a harbi's slave becoming Muslim and coming to dar al-Islam (or conquest). The ruling is that he becomes free immediately.
The same applies if slaves join the Muslim army.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The text cites a hadith: slaves of Ta'if became Muslim and went to the Prophet, who ruled they are free and called them 'utaqa' Allah'. This is a mursal hadith but accepted in Hanafi school.
The rational argument is also given: the slave's own control (yad) over himself is stronger than Muslim control.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's action (qada) and his statement directly indicate automatic freedom; no manumission is required.
The phrase 'utaqa' Allah' suggests divine liberation.
4
Analyze the rational argument
The slave secured himself by his action; his yad is prior and needs only emphasis, while Muslim yad would be new. Hence, his freedom is preferred.
This is a qiyas-like reasoning based on priority of possession.
5
Determine the authority weight
This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah (the core Hanafi texts) and is presented as the relied-upon position without intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
Other schools may differ but the text doesn't discuss them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَ إِذَا أَسْلَمَ عَبْدُ الْحَرْبِيِّ ثُمَّ خَرَجَ إِلَيْنَا أَوْ ظُهِرَ عَلَى الدَّارِ فَهُوَ حُرٌّ وَ كَذَلِكَ إِذَا خَرَجَ عَبِيدُهُمْ إِلَى عَسْكَرِ الْمُسْلِمِينَ فَهُمْ أَحْرَارٌ لِمَا رُوِيَ أَنَّ عَبِيدًا مِنْ عَبِيدِ الطَّائِفِ أَسْلَمُوا وَخَرَجُوا إلى رَسُولِ اللهِ صَلَى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ فَقَضَى بِعِتْقِهِمْ وَقَالَ هُمْ عُتَقَاءُ اللَّهِ وَلِأَنَّهُ أَحْرَزَ نَفْسَهُ بِالْخُرُوجِ إِلَيْنَا مُرَاعَاةً لِمَوْلَاهُ أَوْ بِالْالْتِحَاقِ بِمَنَعَةِ الْمُسْلِمِينَ إِذَا ظُهِرَ عَلَى الدَّارِ وَاعْتِبَارُ يَدِهِ أَوْلَى مِنَ اعْتِبَارِ يَدِ الْمُسْلِمِينَ لِأَنَّهَا أَسْبَقُ ثُبُوتًا عَلَى نَفْسِهِ فَالْحَاجَةُ فِى حَقِّهِ إِلى زِيَادَةِ تَوْكِيدِ وَفِي حَقِّهِمْ إِلَى إِثْبَاتِ الْيَدِ ابْتِدَاءً فَكَانَ أَوْلى
And when a harbi's slave becomes Muslim, then comes out to us (dar al-Islam), or the abode is conquered, then he is free. And similarly, when their slaves go out to the army of the Muslims, they are free. Based on what is narrated that some slaves of the slaves of Ta'if became Muslim and went out to the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), and he ruled that they be freed and said: 'They are the freed ones of Allah.' And because he secured himself by coming out to us out of consideration for his master, or by joining the strength of the Muslims when the abode was conquered. And considering his own (the slave's) control is more appropriate than considering the control of the Muslims, because it is prior in establishment over his own self. So in his right, there is need for additional emphasis, and in their right, there is need for establishing control from the beginning. Therefore, it is more appropriate.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage provides a clear ruling: if a slave belonging to a harbi (non-Muslim from dar al-harb) embraces Islam and then either flees to dar al-Islam or the Muslims conquer the harbi territory, the slave becomes free. The same applies if such slaves join the Muslim army during a campaign. The proof is a hadith about the slaves of Ta'if who became Muslim and came to the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him); he declared them free, calling them 'the freed ones of Allah' (utaqa' Allah). The report is narrated by Ahmad ibn Abi Shaybah, al-Bayhaqi, and al-Tabarani, and Abu Dawud narrated it as mursal. In addition to the hadith, the text provides a rational argument: the slave has secured his own freedom by his action (coming to dar al-Islam or joining the Muslims), and his own control over himself (yaduhu) takes precedence over any claim of ownership by the Muslims (yad al-muslimin). His own control was established first (from his own will), so it only needs reinforcement, whereas the Muslims' control would need to be established anew. Hence, his freedom is preferred.

Key Terms
عُتَقَاءُ اللَّهِ (utaqa' Allah): Those freed by Allah; a term used by the Prophet for slaves who became Muslim and left their masters for Islam.
يَد (yad): Legal possession or control; here, the slave's control over himself (yaduhu) is prioritized over the Muslims' potential control.
ظُهِرَ عَلَى الدَّارِ (zuhira 'ala al-dar): The Muslim conquest of hostile territory (dar al-harb).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary evidence (daleel) for the ruling that a harbi's slave who becomes Muslim and comes to dar al-Islam is free?
A) Caliph Umar's decision
B) The hadith of the slaves of Ta'if
C) A verse from the Qur'an
D) Consensus of the Companions

Key Takeaway
A harbi's slave who becomes Muslim and reaches dar al-Islam (or the territory is conquered) becomes free automatically, proven by the Prophet's hadith and the principle of yad precedence.

Muslim Trader in Dar al-Harb with Aman Cannot Violate Property or Life
Ruling with reasoning that a Muslim trader with aman is forbidden from harming harbis unless the king breaches.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a Muslim trader in dar al-harb with aman, identify the exception, and distinguish it from the captive.

Core Idea
A Muslim's aman creates a binding pact; violating it is treachery, haram, unless the enemy king initiates the breach.

This lesson examines the legal ruling for a Muslim who enters enemy territory as a trader under a grant of safety (aman). We will see why harming the harbis or their property is absolutely forbidden, when an exception applies, and how this differs from the treatment of captives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling and its basis
The text states that it is haram for a Muslim musta'min to interfere with harbi wealth or lives. The basis is that by accepting aman, he guaranteed their safety, and any violation is treachery (ghadr), which is prohibited.
2
Understand the exception
The ruling is lifted if the enemy king himself commits an act of treachery against the Muslims, such as confiscating their goods or detaining them, with his knowledge and without prevention. In that case, the covenant is broken by the king.
The exception requires the king's personal involvement or knowledge and failure to prevent.
3
Contrast with the captive (asir)
A captive does not have aman, so even if released voluntarily, harming them is not considered treachery and is permissible.
This highlights that the prohibition is tied to the existence of a pact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بَابُ الْمُسْتَأْمِنِ وَإِذَا دَخَلَ الْمُسْلِمُ دَارَ الْحَرْبِ تَاجِرًا فَلَا يَحِلُّ لَهُ أَنْ يَتَعَرَّضَ بِشَيْءٍ مِنْ أَمْوَالِهِمْ وَلَا مِنْ دِمَائِهِمْ لِأَنَّهُ ضَمِنَ أَنْ لَا يَتَعَرَّضَ لَهُمْ بِالْإِسْتِئْمَانِ فَالْتَعَرُّضُ بَعْدَ ذَلِكَ يَكُونُ غَدْرًا وَالْغَدْرُ حَرَامٌ إِلَّا إِذَا غَدَرَ بِهِمْ مَلِكُهُمْ فَأَخَذَ أَمْوَالَهُمْ أَوْ حَبَسَهُمْ أَوْ فَعَلَ غَيْرُهُ بِعِلْمِ الْمَلِكِ وَلَمْ يَمْنَعْهُمْ لِأَنَّهُمْ هُمُ الَّذِينَ نَقَضُوا الْعَهْدَ بِخِلَافِ الْأَسِيرِ لِأَنَّهُ غَيْرُ مُسْتَأْمِنٍ فَيُبَاحُ لَهُ التَّعَرُّضُ وَإِنْ أَطْلَقُوهُ طَوْعًا
Chapter of al-Musta'min. And when a Muslim enters dar al-harb as a trader, it is not permissible for him to interfere with anything of their wealth nor with their lives, because he guaranteed (by the aman) that he will not interfere with them. So interfering after that would be treachery (ghadr), and treachery is haram. Except when their king breaks the pact with them (the Muslims) – so he takes their wealth, or detains them, or does other such things with the knowledge of the king and he does not prevent them – because they are the ones who violated the covenant. Unlike the captive (asir), because he is not a musta'min, so it is permissible for him to interfere even if they released him voluntarily.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on al-Musta'min (the one seeking or granted aman). It begins by stating the general rule: a Muslim who enters dar al-harb with an aman (safe conduct) as a trader is absolutely forbidden from violating the lives or property of the harbis. The reason is that by accepting the aman, the Muslim has implicitly guaranteed their safety, and any violation after that constitutes ghadr (treachery), which is prohibited (haram). There is an exception: if the enemy's king (malikuhum) himself commits an act of treachery against the Muslims – for example, by confiscating their goods, detaining them, or committing other hostile acts with the king's knowledge and without him preventing it – then the original pact is considered broken by the king, and the Muslim may reciprocate. This is because the enemy themselves initiated the breach. The text contrasts this with the case of a captive (asir): a captive does not have aman, so even if they are released voluntarily, the Muslim may still harm them (e.g., fight them) because there is no contractual guarantee. Al-Asir is not a musta'min.

Key Terms
مُسْتَأْمِن (musta'min): A person who enters another territory with an aman (safe conduct). In this context, a Muslim trader in dar al-harb.
غَدْر (ghadr): Treachery; breaking a pact or trust after giving assurance of safety.
أَسِير (asir): A captive; a person taken prisoner in war. Unlike a musta'min, he has no aman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition does a Muslim trader in dar al-harb become permitted to harm their property or lives?
A) If any harbi harms a Muslim
B) If the enemy's king betrays the Muslims and does not prevent harm
C) If the trader is a captive
D) If the trader wishes to take spoils

Key Takeaway
A Muslim musta'min must uphold the aman pact; breaking it is treachery unless the enemy king himself breaches first.

Muslim Merchant Entering Dar al-Harb with Aman
Ruling on Muslim entering Dar al-Harb with aman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on the obligations and exceptions of a Muslim merchant who enters Dar al-Harb with aman.

Core Idea
Aman binds a Muslim to refrain from hostility; only the enemy's breach terminates the covenant.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on a Muslim merchant who obtains aman (security pact) to trade in Dar al-Harb. You will learn when the aman obligates non-aggression and when it dissolves due to the enemy's treachery.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary ruling
The Muslim must not molest the wealth or lives of the disbelievers because doing so would be treachery (ghadr).
2
Understand the contractual basis
By taking aman, the Muslim enters a covenant; breach would be treachery, which is haram by ijma.
3
Recognize the exception
If the enemy's king or people betray the Muslim (e.g., seize goods, imprison), the covenant is terminated and the Muslim is no longer bound.
The enemy's own breach nullifies the aman.
4
Contrast with absence of aman
If a Muslim is captured without aman, he may act freely because no covenant exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا دَخَلَ الخ اگر کوئی مسلمان امان لے کر تجارت کی نیت سے دار الحرب پہنچا تو وہ کافروں کے مالوں یا ان کی جانوں سے کچھ تعرض نہ کرے کہ اس کے لئے یہ حلال نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ اس نے امان لے کر یہ عہد کر لیا ہے کہ میں کافروں سے کچھ تعرض ( اور چھیڑ چھاڑ ) نہیں کروں گا ۔ اس لئے اس کے بعد ان سے تعرض کرنا غدر ہے اور بالا جماع غداری کرنا حرام ہے۔ لیکن اگر مسلمان تاجر کے ساتھ ان کفار کا بادشاہ غدر کرے کہ اس کا مال چھین لے یا اس کو قید کرے یا اس بادشاہ کے علم میں ہوتے ہوئے بھی وہاں کے کفار کسی طرح تنگ کریں اور بادشاہ اس کی حفاظت اور ان سے روک پیدا نہ کرے تب اس مسلمان پر بھی اپنے عہد پر قائم رہنا ضروری نہیں رہا کہ یہ عہد ہی ختم ہو چکا ہے۔ اس لئے کہ کافروں نے خود ہی اپنا عہد توڑ دیا ہے۔ اس کے برخلاف اگر کفار کسی مسلمان کو قید کر کے لے جائیں تو وہ جو مناسب سمجھے اپنے لئے کرے کیونکہ اس نے امان کا عہد نامہ ان سے نہیں لیا ہے۔ اس لئے اسے ہر طرح ان سے تعرض کرنا مباح ہوگا۔ اگر چہ کافروں نے اپنی خوشی سے ہی اسے رہا بھی کر دیا ہو۔
And if he enters ... If a Muslim enters Dar al-Harb with aman intending trade, he should not molest the wealth or lives of the disbelievers, for this is not permissible for him. Because by taking aman he has made a covenant that he will not molest them. Hence, after this, molesting them is treachery, and by consensus treachery is haram. But if the king of those disbelievers betrays the Muslim merchant, by taking his wealth or imprisoning him, or if the disbelievers there, with the king's knowledge, harass him and the king does not protect him and prevent them, then it is not necessary for the Muslim to remain bound by his covenant, for the covenant has been terminated because the disbelievers themselves have broken their covenant. In contrast, if disbelievers capture a Muslim and take him away, then he may do what he deems appropriate, because he did not take a covenant of aman from them. Hence, it is permissible for him to molest them in any way, even if the disbelievers release him willingly.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the Hanafi school regarding a Muslim who enters Dar al-Harb with a security pact (aman) for trade. The ruling establishes two key principles: (1) The Muslim must honor the aman and not engage in treachery against the disbelievers, as this would violate the covenant and is haram by consensus. (2) If the disbelievers themselves break the covenant—through their ruler or their actions—the Muslim is no longer bound and may defend himself. However, if a Muslim is captured without aman, he retains full freedom to act against his captors. The passage follows the order: first the prohibition, then the exceptions.

Key Terms
أمان (aman): A security pact or guarantee of safety granted to a non-Muslim entering Muslim territory or a Muslim entering enemy territory.
غدر (ghadr): Treachery or breach of a covenant; the act of violating an aman agreement.
دار الحرب (dar al-harb): Territory under non-Muslim control where Islamic law is not enforced and Muslims may be at risk.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if the king of Dar al-Harb betrays a Muslim merchant who entered with aman?
A) The Muslim must still honor his aman.
B) The Muslim is no longer bound by the aman because the enemy broke the covenant.
C) The Muslim must leave immediately without taking any action.
D) The Muslim may only complain to Muslim authorities.

Key Takeaway
A Muslim who enters Dar al-Harb with aman must honor the covenant; only the enemy's breach releases him.

Ruling on Muslim Betraying and Taking Property from Dar al-Harb
Ruling on a Muslim who commits treachery and brings property to Dar al-Islam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a Muslim who commits treachery and takes property from Dar al-Harb, and explain why ownership is valid but the property must be given in charity.

Core Idea
Ownership from treachery is valid but tainted – the property must be given in charity.

In the context of jihad and aman, the Hanafi school distinguishes between the legal cause (qabd) and the sin of acquisition. Here, a Muslim violates aman, takes enemy property, and brings it to Dar al-Islam. The ruling clarifies that ownership is established because the property itself is permissible and possession is effective, but the means (treachery) imposes a prohibition that requires charity. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this nuanced position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize the act: a Muslim with aman betrays the enemy and takes property. The ruling: he owns it, but with 'ownership prohibited in entry' (mahzur al-wurud), meaning he must give it in charity.
The act is ghadr (treachery), which is haram by consensus.
2
Locate the dalil
The passage relies on ijma (consensus) that treachery is prohibited. No specific verse or hadith is cited; the prohibition is established.
The dalil is the consensus on the prohibition of ghadr.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the prohibition of treachery extend to the property? The ruling states that because the property was obtained through a haram means, a prohibition attaches to it. However, this does not nullify the cause of ownership (qabd).
The prohibition is due to an external factor (ghadr), not the property itself.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may hold that the Muslim does not own the property at all, as the means of acquisition is completely invalid.
The cross-madhhab difference is whether a haram cause prevents ownership entirely.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi position, as stated in the Hidayah, is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view. The property must be given in charity, preserving both the validity of the cause and the requirement to purge the sin.
The tarjih is based on the principle that an external prohibition does not void the contract/cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنْ غَدَرَ بِهِمُ أَعْنِي النَّارَ فَأَخَذَ شَيْئًا وَخَرَجَ بِهِ مَلَكَهُ مِلْكًا مَحْطُورَ الوُرُودِ الْإِسْتِبْلَاءِ عَلَى مَالٍ مُبَاحِ إِلَّا أَنَّهُ حَصَلَ بِسَبَبِ الْعَدْرِ فَأَوْجَبَ ذَاكَ فِيهِ فَيُؤْمَرُ بِالتَصدق به وهذا لأَنَّ الْحَطَرَ لِغَيْرِهِ لَا يَمْنَعُ الْعِقَادَ السَّبَبِ على مابيناه
If he betrays them, meaning the enemy, and takes something and brings it out, he owns it with ownership that is prohibited in entry; it is obtained by usurpation over permissible property, except that it came about by reason of treachery, so that imposes a prohibition in it. So he is commanded to give it in charity. This is because the prohibition due to something else does not prevent the contract of the cause, as we have explained.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage provides a Hanafi ruling on a Muslim who, despite holding aman, commits treachery and takes property from Dar al-Harb and brings it to Dar al-Islam. The ruling: he owns the property, but his ownership is 'mahzur al-wurud' (prohibited in terms of entry) meaning he cannot lawfully benefit from it; he must give it in charity. The legal reasoning (talil) is twofold: first, the property was originally permissible (mubah) and came into his possession through a valid cause (qabd), but second, the means of acquisition (the treachery) is haram, which attaches a prohibition to the property. The principle is that an external prohibition (the sin of ghadr) does not prevent the effectiveness of the cause (the seizure). Thus, ownership is established but tainted, necessitating charity.

Key Terms
غدر (ghadr): Treachery; specifically the breach of aman by a Muslim against the enemy.
محظور الورود (mahzur al-wurud): Prohibited in terms of entry; the ownership is valid but the property cannot be lawfully retained or used.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What must a Muslim do with property taken from Dar al-Harb through treachery?
A) He may keep it as lawful spoils.
B) He must return it to the enemy.
C) He must give it in charity.
D) He must destroy it.

Key Takeaway
When a Muslim acquires permissible property through a haram means, ownership is valid but the property must be given in charity due to the sin of acquisition.

No Judgment for Debts or Usurpation Between Musta'min and Harbi
Ruling on debts and usurpation between a Muslim and Harbi who both become musta'min

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on loans and usurpation between a Muslim and a Harbi who both become musta'min, and explain the reasoning based on jurisdiction and inviolability.

Core Idea
Transactions in Dar al-Harb are not subject to Islamic jurisdiction, even after both parties enter Dar al-Islam.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a critical boundary of Islamic jurisdiction (wilaya). It examines whether debts and usurpation that occurred in enemy territory can be adjudicated once both parties become residents of Dar al-Islam under aman. The Hanafi school rules that no judgment is given, based on two distinct legal reasons: lack of jurisdiction at the time of the transaction, and the non-inviolable nature of Harbi property. Let's explore the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
First, identify the parties: a Muslim with aman enters Dar al-Harb and transacts with a Harbi (loan or usurpation). Later, the Muslim returns to Dar al-Islam and the Harbi becomes a musta'min. The ruling: the qadi does not award anything to either party.
This same ruling applies if two Harbis transact in Dar al-Harb and both later become musta'min.
2
Analyze the reasoning for loans
For loans, the text states that judgment depends on jurisdiction (wilaya). At the time of the loan, no Islamic jurisdiction existed over the parties (they were in Dar al-Harb). Additionally, at the time of judgment, the musta'min is not bound by Islamic law for past actions, only future ones.
Jurisdiction is a condition for enforcement; its absence at the time of transaction prevents retroactive application.
3
Analyze the reasoning for usurpation
For usurpation (ghasb), the property seized in Dar al-Harb is considered ghayr ma'sum (non-inviolable). Therefore, the usurper acquires ownership by taking possession, and no restitution is required even after entering Dar al-Islam.
Harbi property in Dar al-Harb is like mubah (ownerless) from the perspective of Islamic law.
4
Consider the scope and cross-madhhab difference
This ruling is specific to transactions that occurred entirely within Dar al-Harb. Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may argue that once both parties are under Islamic jurisdiction, the transactions become enforceable. The Hanafi position preserves the original legal status.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that Hanafi qadis will dismiss such claims, while other schools may entertain them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا دَخَلَ المُسْلِمُ دَارَ الْحَرْبِ بِأَمَانٍ فَأَدَانَهُ حَرْبِيُّ أَوْ أَدَانَ هُوَ حَرْبِيًّا أَوْ غَصَبَ أَحَدُهُمَا صَاحِبَهُ ثُمَّ خَرَجَ إِلَيْنَا وَاسْتَأَمَنَ الْحَرْبِي لَمْ يُقْضَ لِوَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا عَلَى صَاحِبِهِ بِشَيْءٍ أَمَّا الْإِدَانَةُ فَلِأَنَّ الْقَضَاءَ يَعْتَمِدُ الْوِلَايَةَ وَلَا وِلايَةَ وَقْتَ الْإِدَانَةِ أَصْلاً وَلَا وَقْتَ الْقَضَاءِ عَلَى الْمُسْتَأْمِنِ لأَنَّهُ مَا الْزَمَ حُكْمَ الْإِسْلَامِ فِيْمَا مَضَى مِنْ أَفْعَالِهِ وَإِنَّمَا الْعَزَمَ ذَالِكَ فِى الْمُسْتَقْبَلِ وَأَمَّا الْعَصَبُ فَلِأَنَّهُ صَارَ مِلْكًا لِلَّذِى غَصَبَهُ وَاسْتَوَلَّى عَلَيْهِ لِمُصَادَفَتِهِ مَا لَّا غَيْرَ مَعْصُومٍ عَلَى مَا بَيَّنَّاهُ وَ كَذَالِكَ لَوْ كَانَ حَرْبِيَّشِ فَعَلَا ذَالِكَ ثُمَّ خَرَجًا مُسْتَأْمِنَيْنِ لِمَا قُلْنَا
And if a Muslim enters Dar al-Harb with aman, and a Harbi lends him, or he lends a Harbi, or one of them usurps the other's property, then he comes out to us and the Harbi seeks aman, nothing is awarded to either of them against the other. As for the loan, because judgment depends on jurisdiction, and there was no jurisdiction at the time of the loan, nor at the time of judgment over the musta'min, because he has not made the ruling of Islam binding in past actions, only for the future. And as for usurpation, because the property became owned by the one who usurped it and prevailed over it, since it found something non-inviolable (ghayr ma'sum), as we have explained. Similarly, if they were two Harbis who did that and then both come out as musta'min, [the same applies] for what we have said.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with legal reasoning from the Hanafi school. It deals with two scenarios: (1) A Muslim with aman in Dar al-Harb and a Harbi engage in a loan or usurpation; later the Muslim returns to Dar al-Islam and the Harbi comes as a musta'min. (2) Two Harbis do the same and both become musta'min. In both cases, the judge (qadi) does not enforce any claim between them. The reasoning: For loans, the court's jurisdiction (wilaya) did not exist at the time of the transaction in Dar al-Harb, and the musta'min is not bound by Islamic law for past actions—only for future ones. For usurpation, the seized property becomes owned by the usurper because the property in Dar al-Harb is not inviolable (ghayr ma'sum). Thus, no restitution is ordered.

Key Terms
مستأمن (musta'min): A non-Muslim who enters Muslim territory with a temporary security pact (aman).
ولاية (wilaya): Legal jurisdiction or authority of the qadi to enforce rulings.
غير معصوم (ghayr ma'sum): Non-inviolable; property that is not protected by Islamic law, such as property of Harbis in Dar al-Harb.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the qadi not enforce a loan agreement made in Dar al-Harb between a Muslim and a Harbi when both become musta'min in Dar al-Islam?
A) Because the loan was made without witnesses.
B) Because there was no Islamic jurisdiction at the time of the loan.
C) Because the Harbi is not required to repay Muslims.
D) Because the Muslim has already been forgiven.

Key Takeaway
Islamic jurisdiction does not extend retroactively to transactions that took place in Dar al-Harb, and property seized there becomes owned by the usurper due to its non-inviolable status.

Enforceable Debt After Conversion: Loan vs. Usurpation
Ruling on debts and usurpation after conversion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two Harbis convert to Islam and have a debt or usurpation claim, and explain the reasoning based on contract validity and ownership in Dar al-Harb.

Core Idea
A loan between Harbis is enforceable upon conversion; usurpation is not—ownership already passed in Dar al-Harb.

This lesson examines a specific scenario: two non-Muslims (Harbis) engage in a loan and also an act of usurpation (taking property by force) while in Dar al-Harb. Both later convert to Islam and come before a Hanafi qadi. The passage clarifies which claims the qadi will enforce and why, highlighting the distinction between consensual transactions and non-consensual takings in the context of Dar al-Harb ownership rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two transactions
The passage mentions two distinct acts: a loan (المداينة) and usurpation (الغصب). Recognize that these are treated differently.
Usurpation is forcible taking without consent.
2
State the ruling for each
The qadi adjudicates the debt but does not adjudicate the usurpation.
3
Understand the reasoning for the debt
The loan occurred validly (صحيحة) because it was by mutual consent (بالتراضي). After conversion, both parties are bound by Islamic law, giving the qadi jurisdiction (الولاية ثابتة حالة القضاء).
Validity here means the contract is sound in itself, even if not under Islamic law formalities.
4
Understand the reasoning for usurpation
Usurpation is not enforced because the usurper had already owned the property in Dar al-Harb (ملکه). The Hanafi position holds that property in Dar al-Harb is not inviolable (لا حرمة في ملك الحربي), so there is no claim after conversion.
This is based on the principle that war spoils (ghanima) are permissible; ownership transfers immediately.
5
Recognize the condition for enforcement
Both parties must become Muslims and bind themselves to Islamic rulings (بإلتزامهما الأحكام بالإسلام), which establishes the court's jurisdiction.
If only one converts, the case may be handled differently (not discussed here).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَ لَو خَرَجًا مُسْلِمَيْنِ قُضِيَ بِالدِّيْنِ بَيْنَهُمَا وَلَمْ يُقْضَ بِالْغَضَبِ أَمَّا الْمُدَايَنَهُ فَلِأَنَّهَا وَقَعَتْ صَحِيحَةً لِوُقُوعِهَا بِالتَّرَاضِي وَالْوِلَايَةُ ثَابِتَةُ حَالَةَ الْقَضَاءِ لِإِلْتِرَامِهِمَا الْأَحْكَامُ بِالْإِسْلَامِ وَأَمَّا الْعَصَبُ فَلِمَا بَيْنَا أَنَّهُ مَلَكَهُ وَلَا حُبتَ فِي مِلْكِ الْحَرْبِيِّ حَتَّى يُؤْمَرُ بِالرَّةِ
And if they both come out as Muslims, the debt is adjudicated between them, but usurpation is not adjudicated. As for the loan, because it occurred validly since it was by mutual consent, and jurisdiction is established at the time of judgment due to their binding of the rulings by Islam. As for usurpation, because as we have explained, he owned it and there is no ... in the ownership of the Harbi until he is commanded to return it.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage completes the previous ruling. If two Harbis who had engaged in a loan or usurpation in Dar al-Harb both convert to Islam and come to Dar al-Islam, the qadi enforces the debt because the loan was a valid contract based on mutual consent, and after conversion, both are bound by Islamic law, giving the court jurisdiction. However, usurpation is not enforced because the property was already owned by the usurper (as per Hanafi reasoning that Harbi property is non-inviolable) and the conversion does not retroactively change that. Thus, the victim has no claim.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada'): Judicial enforcement or adjudication by a qadi.
صحيحة (sahihah): Legally sound and valid; the loan contract in Dar al-Harb is considered valid due to mutual consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two Harbis convert to Islam and come to Dar al-Islam, which transaction is enforceable by the qadi?
A) Both loan and usurpation.
B) Only the loan.
C) Only the usurpation.
D) Neither.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school enforces debts contracted in Dar al-Harb when both parties convert to Islam, but does not enforce claims of usurpation because ownership was already transferred in Dar al-Harb.

Seizing Harbi Property Under Aman: Qada vs. Fatwa
Explains ruling on seized property when owner later converts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the dual ruling (qada and fatwa) for a Muslim who seizes harbi property under aman and explain why the qadi does not enforce return but the fatwa advises return.

Core Idea
Legal ownership does not always mean religious permissibility; a corrupt acquisition obligates return despite valid ownership.

This lesson examines a nuanced Hanafi ruling on property acquired during aman. A Muslim enters Dar al-Harb with safe conduct and seizes property. Later, both become Muslim and come to Dar al-Islam. What happens to the property? The answer reveals a key distinction between the qadi's enforcement (qada) and religious counsel (fatwa), rooted in the illicitness of breaking the covenant.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A Muslim enters Dar al-Harb with aman and seizes property from a harbi. Both later become Muslim and exit to Dar al-Islam.
The seizure (ghasb) here is not standard ghasb because the Muslim owns the property through seizure, as per earlier rules.
2
Distinguish qada and fatwa
The text states: 'umira biraddi'l-ghasb wa lam yuqda 'alayh' – he is ordered to return (fatwa) but no judgment is passed against him (qada).
Qada is the enforceable judgment; fatwa is non-enforceable religious advice.
3
Explain the legal basis for qada
The qadi does not enforce return because the Muslim acquired valid ownership through seizure, as previously established.
Ownership (milk) is legally sound in the eyes of the court.
4
Explain the religious basis for fatwa
The ownership is corrupt (fasad) because it accompanied a haram act – breaking the covenant of aman (naqd al-'ahd). Hence, religious obligation to return.
'Illa is the haram act accompanying the acquisition.
5
Note the practical implication
The Muslim must return the property diyaanatan (religiously) but cannot be forced by the court.
This illustrates the Hanafi principle of distinguishing antara1 duniya (legal) and akhirat (religious).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا دَخَلَ الْمُسْلِمُ دَارَ الْحَرْبِ بِأَمَانٍ فَغَصَبَ حَرْبِيًّا ثُمَّ خَرَجَا مُسْلِمَيْنِ أُمِرَ بِرَدِّ الْغَصَبِ وَلَمْ يُقْضَ عَلَيْهِ أَمَّا عَدَمُ الْقَضَاءِ فَلِمَا بَيَّنَّا أَنَّهُ مَلَكَهُ وَأَمَّا الْأَمْرُ بِالرَّدِّ وَمُرَادُهُ الْفَتَوَى بِهِ فَلِأَنَّهُ فَسَدَ الْمِلْكُ لِمَا يُقَارِنُهُ مِنَ الْمُحَرَّمِ وَهُوَ نَقْضُ الْعَهْدِ
ترجمہ اور اگر کوئی مسلمان امان لے کر دار الحرب میں گیا اور اس نے وہاں کسی حربی کا کوئی مال غصب کر لیا پھر وہ حربی مسلمان ہو گیا اور مسلمان کے ساتھ ہو کر دونوں دار الاسلام آگئے ۔ تو دیانت داری کے طور پر اس سے کہا جائے گا کہ اس کا مال مغصوب واپس کر دے۔ مگر قاضی اس کا حکم نہیں کرے گا۔ اور یہ اسی وجہ سے حکم نہیں کرے گا جو اوپر بیان کی جاچکی ہے کہ مسلمان اس محترم مال کا مالک ہوا ہے۔ پھر بھی اس مسلمان کو واپس کرنے کے لئے کہنے اور فتوی دینے کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ وہ غلط اور فاسد طریقہ سے اس مال کا مالک ہوا ہے۔ یعنی اس نے عہد کے خلاف اور غداری کے ساتھ غصب کیا ہے جو ایک حرام کام ہے۔ جس کا تقاضا یہ ہے کہ اصل مالک کو اس کا مال واپس کر دیا جائے۔
And when a Muslim enters Dar al-Harb with aman and seizes property of a harbi, then both exit as Muslims, he is ordered to return the seized property, and judgment is not passed against him. As for the lack of judgment, it is because as we made clear, he owned it. And as for the order to return—its meaning is fatwa to that effect—it is because his ownership became corrupt due to the accompanying haram (prohibited act), which is breaking the covenant. Translation: And if a Muslim entered Dar al-Harb with aman and there he seized some property of a harbi, then that harbi became Muslim and came to Dar al-Islam with the Muslim, then on the basis of piety he will be told to return the seized property. But the qadi will not order it. And the reason he will not order it is as explained above: the Muslim became the owner of that respected property. Nonetheless, the reason for telling the Muslim to return it and giving fatwa (to return) is that he became the owner of that property in a wrong and corrupt manner—i.e., he seized it against the covenant and with treachery, which is a haram act. Its requirement is that the original owner be returned his property.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling (hukm) from the Hanafi school regarding a specific scenario in the context of war and aman (safe conduct). The ruling contains two dimensions: the qadi's enforcement (qada') and religious advice (fatwa). The passage first states that a Muslim who enters Dar al-Harb under aman and seizes property from a harbi (non-Muslim of war territory) is ordered to return it when both parties become Muslim and enter Dar al-Islam. However, the qadi does not enforce this return; the order is a matter of religious counsel. The reason for the qadi's non-enforcement is that the Muslim had validly acquired ownership of the property through seizure, as established earlier in the text. The reason for the religious order to return is that the ownership was acquired through a corrupt means—namely, breaking the covenant of aman, which is haram. Thus, while the ownership is legally valid for the qadi, it is corrupt in the sight of Allah, and the Muslim is religiously obligated to return it. This distinction between legal validity (sihha) and religious acceptability (diyaanatan) is a key Hanafi principle.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasb): Wrongful seizure of another's property without right, in this context the Muslim takes property of a harbi without permission during aman.
أمان (aman): Safe conduct granted to a non-Muslim to enter Dar al-Islam temporarily, or to a Muslim to enter Dar al-Harb. Here it is the latter.
نقض العهد (naqd al-'ahd): Breach of covenant, specifically the violation of the aman agreement by seizing property without right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the difference between the qadi's ruling and the fatwa in the case of a Muslim seizing property from a harbi under aman?
A) The qadi orders return, and the fatwa says keep it.
B) The qadi does not enforce return, but the fatwa advises return.
C) The qadi and fatwa both enforce return.
D) The qadi enforces return, and the fatwa advises keeping it.

Key Takeaway
Legal validity of ownership does not remove religious obligation to return property acquired through breach of aman.

Diyah and Kaffarah for Killing in Dar al-Harb with Aman
Explains rulings for killing in Dar al-Harb with aman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on diyah, kaffarah, and qisas when a Muslim kills another Muslim in Dar al-Harb with aman, and explain the rationale including the absolute command of the Quran and the impossibility of qisas.

Core Idea
A Muslim's inviolability persists in Dar al-Harb, so diyah is due, but qisas is impossible without the Imam's authority.

This passage addresses a unique scenario where two Muslims enter enemy territory under safe conduct and a killing occurs. The Hanafi school derives a combined ruling from the Quran's absolute command for kaffarah and practical constraints for qisas. Understanding this ruling requires distinguishing between intentional and mistaken killing, and recognizing the effect of the change of abode on criminal liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the act
Recognize that two Muslims have entered Dar al-Harb with aman, and one kills the other. The killing can be either intentional or mistaken. The location is enemy territory, not Dar al-Islam.
2
Determine the rulings for each type of killing
For intentional killing: diyah is wajib from the killer's own property, but no qisas. For mistaken killing: diyah from the killer's property plus kaffarah. The 'aqilah are not liable in either case.
Note that qisas is not wajib even in intentional killing according to Abu Hanifah.
3
Trace the proof for kaffarah
The Quranic verse (4:92) commands freeing a slave for mistaken killing in absolute terms, without conditioning on location. According to Hanafi usul, an absolute command applies universally, so kaffarah is required even in Dar al-Harb.
The verse is dalil naqli with qati thubut and dalala.
4
Explain why qisas is not applicable
Qisas requires the authority and power of the Imam and the Muslim community to execute. In Dar al-Harb, such authority is absent, and attempting qisas could lead to fitna. Thus, only diyah is obligatory.
This is a practical obstruction, not a denial of the victim's inviolability.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Abu Hanifah holds no qisas, only diyah. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that qisas is obligatory in intentional killing. The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifah's view as per Qadhi Khan.
Other schools (Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali) agree with the Sahibayn.

ترجمہ وَإِذَا دَخَلَ مُسْلِمَانِ .... الخ اور اگر دو مسلمان امان لے کر دار الحرب میں گئے اور وہاں ایک نے دوسرے کو قتل کر دیا خواہ عمداً ہو یا خطا ہو تو قاتل پر مقتول کی دیت لازم آئے گی لیکن یہ دیت خاص قاتل کے اپنے مال پر واجب ہوگی یعنی اس کی برادری اس میں شریک نہیں ہوگی ۔ اور خطاء کی صورت میں اس پر کفارہ بھی واجب ہو گا ( یعنی عمد قتل کرنے کی صورت میں بھی قتل ( قصاص ) لازم نہیں آئے گا ) بلکہ صرف دیت لازم آئے گی ۔ اور قتل خطاء میں کفارہ اس لئے واجب ہوگا کہ کتاب اللہ میں حکم مطلق ہے ( یعنی کتاب اللہ قرآن مجید میں قتل خطاء کی صورت میں مطلقا کفارہ کا حکم ہے یعنی اس میں اس کی تفصیل نہیں ہے کہ یہ قتل دار الاسلام میں ہو یا دار الحرب میں ہو ۔ پس دار الاسلام ہونے کی کوئی خصوصیت نہیں ہے۔ چنانچہ قرآن مجید میں ہے وَ مَنْ قَتَلَ مُؤْمِنًا خَطَأً فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ مُّؤْمِنَةٍ الايہ یعنی جس نے کسی مومن کو خطا قتل کیا ، اس پر ایک مومن غلام کو آزاد کر نالازم ہوگا الخ اب یہ قتل خواہ دار الاسلام میں ہوا ہو یا دار الحرب میں بہر حال اس پر ایک مؤمن غلام کو آزاد کر نالازم ہو گا ) اور اب قصاص کے حکم کو ختم کر کے دیت لازم کرنے کی وجہ یہ ہوئی ہے کہ مقتول کو دار الاسلام میں رہنے کی وجہ سے اس کی جان کی جو عصمت اور محافظت تھی وہ امان لے کر دار الحرب میں جانے سے ختم نہ ہوئی لہذا اس کا خون یوں ہی ضائع نہیں ہوگا بلکہ اس کا بدل یعنی مال لازم ہوگا۔ اور خون کا بدلہ خون (قصاص ) اس لئے لازم نہ ہو گا کہ قصاص لینے کے لئے طاقتور حکومت اور قدرت کا ہونا ضروری ہے اس کے بغیر یہ ممکن نہ ہوگا ( ہونے سے بھی زبر دست فتنہ پھیل جائے گا ) اور طاقتور حکومت کے لئے امام المؤمنین اور جماعت مسلمین کا ہونا ضروری ہے۔ جبکہ دارالحرب میں اس کا ہونا ممکن نہیں ہے ( ۔ قاضی خان نے ذکر کیا ہے کہ یہ قول امام ابو حنیفہ کا ہے اور صاحبین رحمتہ اللہ علیہا نے کہا ہے کہ عمد ہونے کی صورت میں قاتل پر قصاص لازم ہو گی ۔ یہی قول امام مالک و شافعی واحمد رحمہم اللہ علیہم کا بھی ہے) اس سے پہلے یہی کہا گیا ہے کہ قتل عمد ہونے کی صورت میں جو دیت لازم ہوگی وہ خاص اس قاتل کے مال سے لازم ہوگی۔ اس لئے کہ عاقلہ یعنی مددگار برادری قبل عمد کا جرمانہ اپنے اوپر برداشت نہیں کرتی ہے۔ وہ تو قتل خطا ، میں برداشت کرتی ہے وہ بھی دیت یہاں ان پر لازم نہ ہوگی کیونکہ ان پر دیت اس بناء پر لازم ہوتی ہے کہ وہ لوگ اس خطاوار کو یوں ہی آزاد نہ چھوڑیں بلکہ اس پر نظر رکھیں اور اس کی حفاظت کرتے رہیں ۔ مگر یہاں تو ملک بدلا ہوا ہے یعنی وہ تو دارالحرب ہے جہاں کی طرح بھی اس کی حفاظت ممکن نہیں ہے۔ لہذا ان پر دیت بھی لازم نہ ہوگی۔
And when two Muslims enter Dar al-Harb with aman, then one kills his companion intentionally or by mistake, then the killer owes the diyah from his property, and he owes kaffarah in the case of mistake. As for the kaffarah, it is because of the absolute command of the Book. And the diyah is because the inviolability (ismah) established by secure residence in Dar al-Islam is not invalidated by the incidental entering with aman. And qisas is not obligatory because its execution is only possible with authority, and there is no authority without the Imam and the Muslim community, and that is not found in Dar al-Harb. And diyah is obligatory from his property in case of intent because the 'aqilah (tribal supporters) do not bear intentional killings; and in case of mistake, because they have no ability to protect him given the difference of the two abodes, and the obligation on them is based on the possibility of protection. Translation: And if two Muslims entered Dar al-Harb with aman and there one killed the other, whether intentionally or by mistake, then the killer owes the diyah of the victim from his own property, i.e., his tribe does not share. And in case of mistake, kaffarah is also obligatory. (So in intentional killing, qisas is not obligatory, only diyah.) And the reason kaffarah is obligatory in mistaken killing is that the command in the Book of Allah is absolute—i.e., in the Qur'an, the ruling for mistaken killing is absolute, without specifying whether it occurred in Dar al-Islam or Dar al-Harb. Thus there is no distinction. For Allah says: 'And whoever kills a believer by mistake, then the freeing of a believing slave...' (Surah al-Nisa 4:92). So whether this killing happened in Dar al-Islam or Dar al-Harb, freeing a believing slave is obligatory.) And the reason qisas is replaced by diyah is that the inviolability and protection of the victim's life due to residence in Dar al-Islam did not cease by entering Dar al-Harb with aman. Hence his blood is not wasted; rather, its substitute, i.e., wealth, is obligatory. And blood for blood (qisas) is not obligatory because for qisas a powerful government and authority are necessary; without that it is not possible (and would lead to great fitna). And for a powerful government, the Imam of the believers and the Muslim community are necessary, which is not possible in Dar al-Harb. (Qadhi Khan mentions that this is the view of Imam Abu Hanifah, while the Sahibayn said that in intentional killing, qisas is obligatory. This is also the view of Imam Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad.) Earlier it was stated that in intentional killing, the diyah is from the killer's own property because the 'aqilah do not bear the penalty for intentional acts. They only bear diyah in mistaken killing, and here it is not obligatory on them because diyah is imposed on them on the basis that they would not leave the negligent person free but would watch over and protect him. But here the land is different—it is Dar al-Harb, where protecting him is not possible. Therefore, diyah is not obligatory on them.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where two Muslims are in Dar al-Harb under aman and one kills the other. It covers both intentional and mistaken killing. The ruling consists of several parts: (1) In intentional killing, qisas is not obligatory because it cannot be executed without the power of the Imam and Muslim community, which is absent in Dar al-Harb. Instead, diyah (blood money) is obligatory from the killer's own property, not from his 'aqilah (tribal supporters). (2) In mistaken killing, diyah is also obligatory from the killer's property, and additionally, kaffarah (expiation) is required. The kaffarah is based on the absolute command of the Qur'an (Surah al-Nisa 4:92), which does not distinguish between Dar al-Islam and Dar al-Harb. (3) The 'aqilah are not liable for diyah in either case because in intentional killing they never bear it, and in mistaken killing, their liability is conditional on their ability to provide protection to the killer, which is impossible given the difference of dar (different abodes). The passage also notes an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Abu Hanifah holds that qisas is not obligatory even in intentional killing, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and other imams hold that qisas is obligatory. The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifah, as mentioned by Qadhi Khan.

Key Terms
دية (diyah): Blood money; a financial compensation paid by the killer to the heirs of the victim. Here it is obligatory from the killer's own property.
عصمة (ismah): Inviolability or protected status of a person's life and property. Here it is established by being a Muslim and residing in Dar al-Islam (ihraz).
عاقلہ ('aqilah): The tribal support group or blood-money payers who are liable to pay diyah in cases of mistaken killing. Here they are not liable due to inability to protect in different dar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is kaffarah obligatory in mistaken killing even in Dar al-Harb?
A) Because the verse is absolute and does not distinguish between locations.
B) Because the killer is a Muslim and must always expiate.
C) Because the victim is a Muslim and his inviolability demands it.
D) Because the killer is not punished with qisas, so kaffarah substitutes.

Key Takeaway
A Muslim's inviolability persists in Dar al-Harb, so diyah and kaffarah (for mistake) are due, but qisas is impossible without authority; the Hanafi mufta bihi is no qisas.

Jihad — Armed Conflict Rules

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Diyah for Muslim Prisoners Killing Each Other
Intra-Hanafi dispute on diyah for prisoners

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifah and the Sahibayn on diyah for a Muslim prisoner who kills another Muslim in Dar al-Harb, identify the key evidence for each position, and explain the practical consequence of this dispute.

Core Idea
Subjugation nullifies secure status: a Muslim prisoner in Dar al-Harb owes no diyah for killing another Muslim, only kaffarah.

In this passage, we encounter a classical intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifah and his two chief disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. The question is whether a Muslim prisoner of war who kills another Muslim in enemy territory must pay diyah (blood money). The disagreement hinges on whether captivity completely nullifies the secure status (ihraz) of the prisoner, thereby removing the obligation of diyah. Understanding this debate sharpens your ability to apply the concepts of 'ismah and ihraz in complex scenarios of war and peace.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and the two positions
The case involves two Muslim prisoners in Dar al-Harb, one killing the other (or a merchant killing a prisoner). Note that Abu Hanifah says no diyah (only kaffarah for mistake), while the Sahibayn say diyah is due for both intentional and mistaken killing.
The passage also mentions that qisas is not applicable due to lack of authority (mana'ah).
2
Examine the Sahibayn's reasoning
The Sahibayn argue that captivity does not invalidate the inviolability ('ismah) of the prisoner, just as entering with aman does not. Therefore, diyah is obligatory for both intentional and mistaken killing.
They analogize to the case of a Muslim entering Dar al-Harb with aman, where diyah is due.
3
Examine Abu Hanifah's reasoning
Abu Hanifah argues that captivity involves complete subjugation (qahr) to the enemy, making the prisoner a tabi' (subordinate) in residence and travel. This nullifies ihraz (secure status) entirely, similar to a Muslim who never migrated to Dar al-Islam. Thus, no diyah is owed; only kaffarah in mistaken killing.
He distinguishes captivity from aman, where subjugation is absent.
4
Compare the key difference
The core difference is whether captivity nullifies ihraz. Abu Hanifah says it does (due to qahr), while the Sahibayn say it does not (only a temporary incidental state).
This difference hinges on the legal effect of being under enemy control.
5
Determine the practical consequence
According to Abu Hanifah, the killer pays only kaffarah for mistake (nothing for intentional). According to the Sahibayn, the killer pays diyah (from his own property, not 'aqilah) plus kaffarah for mistake. The mufta bihi (relied-upon) position in the Hanafi school is typically Abu Hanifah's view.
The financial liability of the killer and compensation to the victim's heirs differs.
